   <h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en"><span dir="auto">University of Pennsylvania</span></h1> 
   <div id="bodyContent"> 
    <div id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div> 
    <div id="contentSub"></div>

     <div class="dablink">
      This article is about the private Philadelphia-based Ivy League university colloquially referred to as &quot;Penn&quot;. For the public university located in State College, Pennsylvania, and colloquially known as &quot;Penn State&quot;, see 
      <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_State_University" title="Pennsylvania State University">Pennsylvania State University</a>.
     </div>

     <p>The <b>University of Pennsylvania</b> (commonly referred to as <b>Penn</b> or <b>UPenn</b>) is an American <a href="/wiki/Private_university" title="Private university">private</a> <a href="/wiki/Ivy_League" title="Ivy League">Ivy League</a> <a href="/wiki/Doctoral_university" title="Doctoral university" class="mw-redirect">research university</a> located in <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a>, Pennsylvania, United States. It is one of the <a href="/wiki/Colonial_Colleges" title="Colonial Colleges" class="mw-redirect">Colonial Colleges</a>. Incorporated as <i>The Trustees of the University of Pennsylvania</i>, Penn is one of 14 founding members of the <a href="/wiki/Association_of_American_Universities" title="Association of American Universities">Association of American Universities</a>.</p> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin" title="Benjamin Franklin">Benjamin Franklin</a>, Penn's <a href="/wiki/Entrepreneur" title="Entrepreneur">founder</a>, advocated an educational program that focused as much on practical education for commerce and <a href="/wiki/Public_services" title="Public services" class="mw-redirect">public service</a> as on the classics and theology. Penn was one of the first academic institutions to follow a <a href="/wiki/Multidisciplinary" title="Multidisciplinary" class="mw-redirect">multidisciplinary</a> model pioneered by several European universities, concentrating multiple &quot;faculties&quot; (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">theology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Classics" title="Classics">classics</a>, medicine) into one institution.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup> It was also home to many other educational <a href="/wiki/Innovations" title="Innovations" class="mw-redirect">innovations</a>. The first school of medicine in North America (<a href="/wiki/Perelman_School_of_Medicine_at_the_University_of_Pennsylvania" title="Perelman School of Medicine at the University of Pennsylvania">Perelman School of Medicine</a>, 1765), the first collegiate business school (<a href="/wiki/Wharton_School_of_the_University_of_Pennsylvania" title="Wharton School of the University of Pennsylvania">Wharton</a>, 1881) and the first <a href="/wiki/Student_activity_center" title="Student activity center">student union</a> (<a href="/wiki/Houston_Hall_(University_of_Pennsylvania)" title="Houston Hall (University of Pennsylvania)">Houston Hall</a>, 1896),<sup id="cite_ref-University_Archives_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-University_Archives-7"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> were all born at Penn.</p> 
     <p>Penn offers a broad range of academic departments, an extensive research enterprise and a number of <a href="/wiki/Community_outreach" title="Community outreach" class="mw-redirect">community outreach</a> and public service programs. It is particularly well known for its <a href="/wiki/Perelman_School_of_Medicine_at_the_University_of_Pennsylvania" title="Perelman School of Medicine at the University of Pennsylvania">medical school</a>, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Pennsylvania_School_of_Design" title="University of Pennsylvania School of Design">design school</a>, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Pennsylvania_School_of_Dental_Medicine" title="University of Pennsylvania School of Dental Medicine">dental school</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wharton_School_of_the_University_of_Pennsylvania" title="Wharton School of the University of Pennsylvania">school of business</a>, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Pennsylvania_Law_School" title="University of Pennsylvania Law School">law school</a>, <a href="/wiki/Annenberg_School_for_Communication_at_the_University_of_Pennsylvania" title="Annenberg School for Communication at the University of Pennsylvania">communications school</a>, nursing school, veterinary school, its <a href="/wiki/Social_sciences" title="Social sciences" class="mw-redirect">social sciences</a> and <a href="/wiki/Humanities" title="Humanities">humanities</a> programs, as well as its <a href="/wiki/Biomedical" title="Biomedical" class="mw-redirect">biomedical</a> teaching and research capabilities. Its undergraduate programs are also among the most selective in the country (12.1% acceptance rate).<sup id="cite_ref-Daily_Pennsylvanian_Admission_rate_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Daily_Pennsylvanian_Admission_rate-8"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> One of Penn's most well known academic qualities is its emphasis on interdisciplinary education, which it promotes through numerous <a href="/wiki/Joint_degree" title="Joint degree" class="mw-redirect">joint degree</a> programs, research centers and <a href="/wiki/Professorships" title="Professorships" class="mw-redirect">professorships</a>, a unified campus, and the ability for students to take classes from any of Penn's schools (the &quot;One University Policy&quot;).<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>All of Penn's schools, alone or jointly, exhibit very high research activity. Penn is consistently included among the top five research universities in the United States,<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> and among the top research universities in the world, for both quality and quantity of research.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Fiscal_year" title="Fiscal year">fiscal year</a> 2011, Penn topped the Ivy League in academic research spending with an $814&nbsp;million <a href="/wiki/Budget" title="Budget">budget</a>, involving some 4,000 faculty, 1,100 postdoctoral fellows and 5,400 support staff/graduate assistants.<sup id="cite_ref-Penn:_Penn_Facts_3-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Penn:_Penn_Facts-3"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup> As one of the most active and prolific research institutions, Penn is associated with several important innovations and discoveries in many fields of science and the humanities. Among them are the first general purpose electronic computer (<a href="/wiki/ENIAC" title="ENIAC">ENIAC</a>), the <a href="/wiki/Rubella" title="Rubella">Rubella</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_B" title="Hepatitis B">Hepatitis B</a> vaccines, <a href="/wiki/Retin-A" title="Retin-A" class="mw-redirect">Retin-A</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_therapy" title="Cognitive therapy">cognitive therapy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Conjoint_analysis" title="Conjoint analysis">conjoint analysis</a> and others.</p> 
     <p>Penn's academic and research programs are led by a large and highly productive faculty.<sup id="cite_ref-chronicle.com_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chronicle.com-12"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> In the last ten years alone 9 Penn faculty members or graduates have won a <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize" title="Nobel Prize">Nobel Prize</a>. Over its long history the university has also produced many distinguished alumni. These include 12 heads of state (including one U.S. President), 3 <a href="/wiki/United_States_Supreme_Court" title="United States Supreme Court" class="mw-redirect">United States Supreme Court</a> justices, and supreme court justices of other states, founders of technology companies, international law firms, and global financial institutions, university presidents and 18 living billionaires.<sup id="cite_ref-Billionaire_Universities_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Billionaire_Universities-13"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <table id="toc" class="toc"> 
      <tbody>
       <tr>

     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Pennsylvania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <table class="metadata plainlinks ambox ambox-content ambox-POV" style=""> 
      <tbody>
       <tr> 
        <td class="mbox-image"> 
         <div style="width: 52px;">
          <img alt="Unbalanced scales.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Unbalanced_scales.svg/45px-Unbalanced_scales.svg.png" width="45" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Unbalanced_scales.svg/68px-Unbalanced_scales.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Unbalanced_scales.svg/90px-Unbalanced_scales.svg.png 2x" />
         </div> </td> 
        <td class="mbox-text" style=""><span class="mbox-text-span">The <b><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Neutral_point_of_view" title="Wikipedia:Neutral point of view">neutrality</a> of this article is <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NPOV_dispute" title="Wikipedia:NPOV dispute">disputed</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Relevant discussion may be found on the <a href="/wiki/Talk:University_of_Pennsylvania#.23" title="Talk:University of Pennsylvania">talk page</a>. Please do not remove this message until the <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NPOVD#What_is_an_NPOV_dispute.3F" title="Wikipedia:NPOVD" class="mw-redirect">dispute is resolved</a>.</span> <small><i>(June 2013)</i></small> </span></td> 
       </tr> 
      </tbody>
     </table> 
     <p>The school considers itself the fourth-oldest institution of <a href="/wiki/Higher_education" title="Higher education">higher education</a> in the United States,<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span>[</span>note 2<span>]</span></a></sup> as well as the <a href="/wiki/First_university_in_the_United_States" title="First university in the United States">first university in the United States</a> with both undergraduate and graduate studies.</p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Benjamin_Franklin_statue_in_front_of_College_Hall.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Benjamin_Franklin_statue_in_front_of_College_Hall.JPG/220px-Benjamin_Franklin_statue_in_front_of_College_Hall.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Benjamin_Franklin_statue_in_front_of_College_Hall.JPG/330px-Benjamin_Franklin_statue_in_front_of_College_Hall.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Benjamin_Franklin_statue_in_front_of_College_Hall.JPG/440px-Benjamin_Franklin_statue_in_front_of_College_Hall.JPG 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Benjamin_Franklin_statue_in_front_of_College_Hall.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> This statue of 
        <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin" title="Benjamin Franklin">Benjamin Franklin</a> donated by Justus C. Strawbridge to the City of 
        <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a> in 1899 now sits in front of 
        <a href="/wiki/College_Hall_(University_of_Pennsylvania)" title="College Hall (University of Pennsylvania)">College Hall</a>.
        <sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>In 1740, a group of Philadelphians joined together to erect a great preaching hall for the traveling <a href="/wiki/Evangelism" title="Evangelism">evangelist</a> <a href="/wiki/George_Whitefield" title="George Whitefield">George Whitefield</a>, who toured the American colonies delivering open air sermons. The building was designed and built by <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Woolley" title="Edmund Woolley">Edmund Woolley</a> and was the largest building in the city at the time. It was initially planned to serve as a <a href="/wiki/Charity_school" title="Charity school">charity school</a> as well; however, a lack of funds forced plans for the chapel and school to be suspended. In the fall of 1749, eager to create a school to educate future generations, <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin" title="Benjamin Franklin">Benjamin Franklin</a> circulated a pamphlet titled &quot;Proposals for the Education of Youth in Pennsylvania,&quot; his vision for what he called a &quot;Public Academy of Philadelphia.&quot;<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> However, according to Franklin's autobiography, it was in 1743 when he first had the idea to establish an academy, &quot;thinking the Rev. <a href="/wiki/Richard_Peters_(cleric)" title="Richard Peters (cleric)">Richard Peters</a> a fit person to superintend such an institution.&quot; Unlike the other <a href="/wiki/Colonial_colleges" title="Colonial colleges" class="mw-redirect">Colonial colleges</a> that existed in 1743?<a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard</a>, <a href="/wiki/College_of_William_and_Mary" title="College of William and Mary" class="mw-redirect">William and Mary</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Yale" title="Yale" class="mw-redirect">Yale</a>?Franklin's new school would not focus merely on education for the clergy. He advocated an innovative concept of higher education, one which would teach both the ornamental knowledge of the arts and the practical skills necessary for making a living and doing public service. The proposed program of study could have become the nation's first modern liberal arts curriculum, although it was never implemented because provost <a href="/wiki/William_Smith_(Anglican_priest)" title="William Smith (Anglican priest)" class="mw-redirect">William Smith</a> and other trustees preferred the traditional curriculum.<sup id="cite_ref-Penn.27s_Heritage_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Penn.27s_Heritage-17"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Franklin assembled a board of trustees from among the leading citizens of Philadelphia, the first such non-sectarian board in America. At the first meeting of the 24 members of the Board of Trustees (November 13, 1749) the issue of where to locate the school was a prime concern. Although a lot across Sixth Street from <a href="/wiki/Independence_Hall_(United_States)" title="Independence Hall (United States)" class="mw-redirect">Independence Hall</a> was offered without cost by <a href="/wiki/James_Logan_(statesman)" title="James Logan (statesman)">James Logan</a>, its owner, the Trustees realized that the building erected in 1740, which was still vacant, would be an even better site. The original sponsors of the dormant building still owed considerable construction debts and asked Franklin's group to assume their debts and, accordingly, their inactive trusts. On February 1, 1750 the new board took over the building and trusts of the old board. On August 13, 1751 the Academy of Philadelphia, using the great hall at 4th and Arch Streets, took in its first secondary students. A charity school also was opened in accordance with the intentions of the original &quot;New Building&quot; donors, although it lasted only a few years. In 1755, the <a href="/wiki/College_of_Philadelphia" title="College of Philadelphia" class="mw-redirect">College of Philadelphia</a> was chartered, paving the way for the addition of undergraduate instruction. All three schools shared the same Board of Trustees and were considered to be part of the same institution.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-18"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Penn_campus_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Penn_campus_2.jpg/220px-Penn_campus_2.jpg" width="220" height="116" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Penn_campus_2.jpg/330px-Penn_campus_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Penn_campus_2.jpg/440px-Penn_campus_2.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Penn_campus_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Quad in the Fall, facing Ware College House
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The institution of higher learning was known as the College of Philadelphia from 1755 to 1779. In 1779, not trusting then-provost the <a href="/wiki/William_Smith_(Anglican_priest)" title="William Smith (Anglican priest)" class="mw-redirect">Rev. William Smith's</a> <a href="/wiki/Loyalist_(American_Revolution)" title="Loyalist (American Revolution)">loyalist</a> tendencies, the revolutionary State Legislature created a <a href="/wiki/University_of_the_State_of_Pennsylvania" title="University of the State of Pennsylvania">University of the State of Pennsylvania</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-18"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup> The result was a schism, with Smith continuing to operate an attenuated version of the College of Philadelphia. In 1791 the legislature issued a new charter, merging the two institutions into the University of Pennsylvania with twelve men from each institution on the new board of trustees.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Penn has three claims to being the <a href="/wiki/First_university_in_the_United_States" title="First university in the United States">first university in the United States</a>, according to university archives director Mark Frazier Lloyd: the 1765 founding of the first medical school in America<sup id="cite_ref-WDL1_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WDL1-20"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup> made Penn the first institution to offer both &quot;undergraduate&quot; and professional education; the 1779 charter made it the first American institution of higher learning to take the name of &quot;University&quot;; and existing colleges were established as seminaries.<sup id="cite_ref-first_university_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-first_university-21"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>After being located in downtown Philadelphia for more than a century, the campus was moved across the <a href="/wiki/Schuylkill_River" title="Schuylkill River">Schuylkill River</a> to property purchased from the <a href="/wiki/Blockley_Almshouse" title="Blockley Almshouse">Blockley Almshouse</a> in <a href="/wiki/West_Philadelphia" title="West Philadelphia">West Philadelphia</a> in 1872, where it has since remained in an area now known as <a href="/wiki/University_City,_Philadelphia,_Pennsylvania" title="University City, Philadelphia, Pennsylvania" class="mw-redirect">University City</a>. Although Penn began operating as an academy or secondary school in 1751 and obtained its collegiate charter in 1755, it initially designated 1750 as its founding date; this is the year which appears on the first iteration of the university seal. Sometime later in its early history, Penn began to consider 1749 as its founding date; this year was referenced for over a century, including at the centennial celebration in 1849.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1899, the board of trustees voted to adjust the founding date earlier again, this time to 1740, the date of &quot;the creation of the earliest of the many educational trusts the University has taken upon itself.&quot;<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup> The board of trustees voted in response to a three-year campaign by Penn's General Alumni Society to retroactively revise the university's founding date to appear older than Princeton University, which had been chartered in 1746.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Educational_innovations">Educational innovations</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Pennsylvania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Educational innovations">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Universityofpennsylvaniacollegehall.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/68/Universityofpennsylvaniacollegehall.jpeg/220px-Universityofpennsylvaniacollegehall.jpeg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/68/Universityofpennsylvaniacollegehall.jpeg/330px-Universityofpennsylvaniacollegehall.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/6/68/Universityofpennsylvaniacollegehall.jpeg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Universityofpennsylvaniacollegehall.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> College Hall and Cohen Hall viewed from Woodland Ave., ca. 1892
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Penn's educational innovations include: the nation's first medical school in 1765; the first university teaching hospital in 1874; the <a href="/wiki/Wharton_School_of_the_University_of_Pennsylvania" title="Wharton School of the University of Pennsylvania">Wharton School</a>, the world's first collegiate school of business, in 1881; the first American student union building, <a href="/wiki/Houston_Hall_(University_of_Pennsylvania)" title="Houston Hall (University of Pennsylvania)">Houston Hall</a>, in 1896;<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup> the country's second school of veterinary medicine; and the home of <a href="/wiki/ENIAC" title="ENIAC">ENIAC</a>, the world's first electronic, large-scale, general-purpose digital computer in 1946. Penn is also home to the oldest continuously functioning <a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">psychology</a> department in North America and is where the <a href="/wiki/American_Medical_Association" title="American Medical Association">American Medical Association</a> was founded.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup> Penn was also the first university to award a PhD to an African-American woman, <a href="/wiki/Sadie_Tanner_Mossell_Alexander" title="Sadie Tanner Mossell Alexander">Sadie Tanner Mossell Alexander</a>, in 1921 (in economics).<sup id="cite_ref-Almanac_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Almanac-27"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Motto">Motto</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Pennsylvania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Motto">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>Penn's motto is based on a line from <a href="/wiki/Horace" title="Horace">Horace</a>?s III.24 (Book 3, Ode 24), <i>quid leges sine moribus vanae proficiunt?</i> (&quot;of what avail empty laws without [good] morals?&quot;) From 1756 to 1898, the motto read <i>Sine Moribus Vanae</i>. When it was pointed out that the motto could be translated as &quot;Loose women without morals,&quot; the university quickly changed the motto to <i>literae sine moribus vanae</i> (&quot;Letters without morals [are] useless&quot;). In 1932, all elements of the seal were revised, and as part of the redesign it was decided that the new motto &quot;mutilated&quot; Horace, and it was changed to its present wording, <i>Leges Sine Moribus Vanae</i> (&quot;Laws without morals [are] useless&quot;).<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Seal">Seal</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Pennsylvania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Seal">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright" style="width: 217px;"> 
      <div class="thumbinner"> 
       <div style="float: left; margin: 1px; width: 101px"> 
        <div class="thumbimage">
         <a href="/wiki/File:1757_UPenn_Seal.png" class="image"><img alt="1757 Seal of the Trustees of the University of Pennsylvania" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/1757_UPenn_Seal.png/99px-1757_UPenn_Seal.png" width="99" height="100" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/1757_UPenn_Seal.png/149px-1757_UPenn_Seal.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/1757_UPenn_Seal.png/198px-1757_UPenn_Seal.png 2x" /></a>
        </div> 
        <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
         1757?1780
        </div> 
       </div> 
       <div style="float: left; margin: 1px; width: 104px"> 
        <div class="thumbimage">
         <a href="/wiki/File:U_Pennsylvania_Seal.gif" class="image"><img alt="Current Seal of the Trustees of the University of Pennsylvania" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/ff/U_Pennsylvania_Seal.gif/102px-U_Pennsylvania_Seal.gif" width="102" height="100" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/ff/U_Pennsylvania_Seal.gif/153px-U_Pennsylvania_Seal.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/ff/U_Pennsylvania_Seal.gif/204px-U_Pennsylvania_Seal.gif 2x" /></a>
        </div> 
        <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
         1933 ? present
        </div> 
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The official <a href="/wiki/Seal_(emblem)" title="Seal (emblem)">seal</a> of the Trustees of the University of Pennsylvania serves as the signature and symbol of authenticity on documents issued by the corporation.<sup id="cite_ref-sealFAQ_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sealFAQ-29"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup> A request for one was first recorded in a meeting of the trustees in 1753 during which some of the Trustees ?desired to get a Common Seal engraved for the Use of [the] Corporation.? However, it was not until a meeting in 1756 that ?a public Seal for the College with a proper device and Motto? was requested to be engraved in silver.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup> The most recent design, a modified version of the original seal, was approved in 1932, adopted a year later, and is still used for much of the same purposes as the original.<sup id="cite_ref-sealFAQ_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sealFAQ-29"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The outer ring of the current seal is inscribed with ?Universitas Pennsylvaniensis,? the Latin name of the University of Pennsylvania. The inside contains seven stacked books on a desk with the titles of what was the common curriculum in 1932: Theolog, Astronom, Philosoph, Mathemat, Logica, Rhetorica, Grammatica. Between the books and the outer ring is the Latin motto of the University, ?Leges Sine Moribus Vanae.?<sup id="cite_ref-sealFAQ_29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sealFAQ-29"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Campus">Campus</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Pennsylvania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Campus">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Foliage_at_Penn_2005_035.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Foliage_at_Penn_2005_035.jpg/220px-Foliage_at_Penn_2005_035.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Foliage_at_Penn_2005_035.jpg/330px-Foliage_at_Penn_2005_035.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Foliage_at_Penn_2005_035.jpg/440px-Foliage_at_Penn_2005_035.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Foliage_at_Penn_2005_035.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Overlooking Lower Quad from Upper Quad
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Much of Penn's architecture was designed by the <a href="/wiki/Cope_%26_Stewardson" title="Cope &amp; Stewardson" class="mw-redirect">Cope &amp; Stewardson</a> firm, whose principal architects combined the <a href="/wiki/Gothic_architecture" title="Gothic architecture">Gothic architecture</a> of the University of Oxford and the University of Cambridge with the local landscape to establish the <a href="/wiki/Gothic_Revival_architecture" title="Gothic Revival architecture">Collegiate Gothic</a> style. The present core campus covers over 279 acres (1.13&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup>) in a contiguous area of West Philadelphia's University City district; the older heart of the campus comprises the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Pennsylvania_Campus_Historic_District" title="University of Pennsylvania Campus Historic District">University of Pennsylvania Campus Historic District</a>. All of Penn's schools and most of its research institutes are located on this campus. The surrounding neighborhood includes several restaurants and pubs, a large upscale grocery store, and a movie theater on the western edge of campus.</p> 
     <p>The Module 6 Utility Plant and Garage at Penn was designed by BLT Architects and completed in 1995. Module 6 is located at 38th &amp; Walnut and includes spaces for 627 vehicles, 9,000&nbsp;sq&nbsp;ft (840&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>) of storefront retail operations, a 9,500-ton chiller module and corresponding extension of the campus chilled water loop, and a 4,000-ton ice storage facility.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 2007, Penn acquired about 35 acres (140,000&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>) between the campus and the <a href="/wiki/Schuylkill_River" title="Schuylkill River">Schuylkill River</a> (the former site of the <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_Civic_Center" title="Philadelphia Civic Center" class="mw-redirect">Philadelphia Civic Center</a> and a nearby 24-acre (97,000&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>) site owned by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Postal_Service" title="United States Postal Service">United States Postal Service</a>). Dubbed the Postal Lands, the site extends from Market Street on the north to Penn's Bower Field on the south, including the former main regional U.S. Postal Building at 30th and Market Streets, now the regional office for the U.S. <a href="/wiki/Internal_Revenue_Service" title="Internal Revenue Service">Internal Revenue Service</a>. Over the next decade, the site will become the home to educational, research, <a href="/wiki/Biomedical_engineering" title="Biomedical engineering">biomedical</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mixed-use_development" title="Mixed-use development">mixed-use</a> facilities. The first phase, a comprising a park and athletic facilities, opened in the fall of 2011. Penn also plans new connections between the campus and the city, including a pedestrian bridge. In 2010 Penn, in its first significant expansion across the <a href="/wiki/Schuylkill_River" title="Schuylkill River">Schuylkill River</a>, purchased 23 acres at the northwest corner of 34th Street and Grays Ferry Avenue from <a href="/wiki/DuPont" title="DuPont">DuPont</a> for storage and office space.</p> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:UQG.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/UQG.jpg/220px-UQG.jpg" width="220" height="113" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/UQG.jpg/330px-UQG.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/UQG.jpg/440px-UQG.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:UQG.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Upper Quad Gate
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>In September 2011 Penn completed the construction of the $46.5 million 24-acre (97,000&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>) Penn Park, which features passive and active recreation and athletic components framed and subdivided by canopy trees, lawns, and meadows. It is located east of the Highline Green and stretches from Walnut Street to South Streets. The University also owns the 92-acre (370,000&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>) <a href="/wiki/Morris_Arboretum" title="Morris Arboretum">Morris Arboretum</a> in <a href="/wiki/Chestnut_Hill,_Philadelphia,_Pennsylvania" title="Chestnut Hill, Philadelphia, Pennsylvania" class="mw-redirect">Chestnut Hill</a> in northwestern Philadelphia, the official arboretum of the state of <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a>. Penn also owns the 687-acre (2.78&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup>) <a href="/wiki/New_Bolton_Center" title="New Bolton Center">New Bolton Center</a>, the research and large-animal health care center of its Veterinary School. Located near <a href="/wiki/Kennett_Square,_Pennsylvania" title="Kennett Square, Pennsylvania">Kennett Square</a>, New Bolton Center received nationwide media attention when <a href="/wiki/Kentucky_Derby" title="Kentucky Derby">Kentucky Derby</a> winner <a href="/wiki/Barbaro" title="Barbaro">Barbaro</a> underwent surgery at its Widener Hospital for injuries suffered while running in the <a href="/wiki/Preakness_Stakes" title="Preakness Stakes">Preakness Stakes</a>.</p> 
     <p>Penn borders <a href="/wiki/Drexel_University" title="Drexel University">Drexel University</a> and is near the <a href="/wiki/University_of_the_Sciences_in_Philadelphia" title="University of the Sciences in Philadelphia" class="mw-redirect">University of the Sciences in Philadelphia</a>. The renowned cancer research center <a href="/wiki/Wistar_Institute" title="Wistar Institute">Wistar Institute</a> is also located on campus. In 2014 a new 7-story glass and steel building will be completed next to the Institute's historic 117-year-old brick building further expanding collaboration between the university and the Wistar Institute.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Libraries">Libraries</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Pennsylvania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Libraries">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:FisherFineArtsLibrary.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/FisherFineArtsLibrary.JPG/220px-FisherFineArtsLibrary.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/FisherFineArtsLibrary.JPG/330px-FisherFineArtsLibrary.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/FisherFineArtsLibrary.JPG/440px-FisherFineArtsLibrary.JPG 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:FisherFineArtsLibrary.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> 
        <a href="/wiki/Fisher_Fine_Arts_Library" title="Fisher Fine Arts Library">Fisher Fine Arts Library</a>, also referred to as the Furness Library or simply the Fine Arts Library
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Penn's library began in 1750 with a donation of books from cartographer Lewis Evans. Twelve years later, then-provost <a href="/wiki/William_Smith_(Anglican_priest)" title="William Smith (Anglican priest)" class="mw-redirect">William Smith</a> sailed to England to raise additional funds to increase the collection size. More than 250 years later, it has grown into a system of 15 libraries (13 are on the contiguous campus) with 400 <a href="/wiki/Full-time_equivalent" title="Full-time equivalent">full-time equivalent</a> (FTE) employees and a total operating budget of more than $48&nbsp;million. The library system holds 6.01&nbsp;million book and serial volumes as well as 4.21&nbsp;million <a href="/wiki/Microform" title="Microform">microform</a> items.<sup id="cite_ref-Penn:_Penn_Facts_3-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Penn:_Penn_Facts-3"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup> It subscribes to over 68,000 print serials and e-journals.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Penn's Libraries, with associated school or subject area: Annenberg (School of Communications), located in the Annenberg School; Biddle (Law), located in the Law School; Biomedical, located adjacent to the Robert Wood Johnson Pavilion of the Medical School; Chemistry, located in the 1973 Wing of the Chemistry Building; Dental Medicine; Engineering, located on the second floor of the Towne Building in the Engineering School; Fine Arts, located within the <a href="/wiki/Fisher_Fine_Arts_Library" title="Fisher Fine Arts Library">Fisher Fine Arts Library</a>, designed by <a href="/wiki/Frank_Furness" title="Frank Furness">Frank Furness</a>; Katz Center for Advanced Judaic Studies, located on Walnut Street at Washington Square; Lea Library, located within the Van Pelt Library; Lippincott (Wharton School), located on the second floor of the Van Pelt-Dietrich Library Center; Math/Physics/Astronomy, located on the third floor of David Rittenhouse Laboratory; Museum (Anthropology); Rare Books and Manuscripts; <a href="/wiki/Van_Pelt_Library" title="Van Pelt Library">Van Pelt-Dietrich Library Center</a> (Humanities and Social Sciences) ? location of <a href="/wiki/Weigle_Information_Commons" title="Weigle Information Commons" class="mw-redirect">Weigle Information Commons</a>; Veterinary Medicine, located in Penn Campus and New Bolton Center; and High Density Storage.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_University_Museum">The University Museum</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Pennsylvania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: The University Museum">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:University_of_Pennsylvania_Museum_of_Archaeology_and_Anthropology.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/University_of_Pennsylvania_Museum_of_Archaeology_and_Anthropology.JPG/220px-University_of_Pennsylvania_Museum_of_Archaeology_and_Anthropology.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/University_of_Pennsylvania_Museum_of_Archaeology_and_Anthropology.JPG/330px-University_of_Pennsylvania_Museum_of_Archaeology_and_Anthropology.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/University_of_Pennsylvania_Museum_of_Archaeology_and_Anthropology.JPG/440px-University_of_Pennsylvania_Museum_of_Archaeology_and_Anthropology.JPG 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:University_of_Pennsylvania_Museum_of_Archaeology_and_Anthropology.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> University Museum and Warden Garden
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/University_of_Pennsylvania_Museum_of_Archaeology_and_Anthropology" title="University of Pennsylvania Museum of Archaeology and Anthropology">University of Pennsylvania Museum of Archaeology and Anthropology</a>
     </div> 
     <p>Since the University museum was founded in 1887, it has taken part in 400 research projects worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-About_UPM_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-About_UPM-34"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup> The museum's first project was an excavation of <a href="/wiki/Nippur" title="Nippur">Nippur</a>, a location in current day Iraq.<sup id="cite_ref-Penn_museum_research_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Penn_museum_research-35"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup> The museum has three gallery floors with artifacts from <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, the Middle East, <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerica" title="Mesoamerica">Mesoamerica</a>, Asia, the Mediterranean, Africa, and indigenous artifacts of the Americas.<sup id="cite_ref-About_UPM_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-About_UPM-34"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup> Its most famous object is the goat rearing into the branches of a rosette-leafed plant, from the <a href="/wiki/Ur_III" title="Ur III" class="mw-redirect">royal tombs of Ur</a>. The Museum's excavations and collections foster a strong research base for graduate students in the <a href="/wiki/Graduate_Group_in_the_Art_and_Archaeology_of_the_Mediterranean_World" title="Graduate Group in the Art and Archaeology of the Mediterranean World">Graduate Group in the Art and Archaeology of the Mediterranean World</a>. Features of the <a href="/wiki/Beaux-Arts_architecture" title="Beaux-Arts architecture">Beaux-Arts</a> building include a <a href="/wiki/Rotunda_(architecture)" title="Rotunda (architecture)">rotunda</a> and gardens that include Egyptian <a href="/wiki/Papyrus" title="Papyrus">papyrus</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Institute_of_Contemporary_Art,_Philadelphia" title="Institute of Contemporary Art, Philadelphia">Institute of Contemporary Art</a>, which is based on Penn's campus, showcases various art exhibitions throughout the year.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Residences">Residences</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Pennsylvania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Residences">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/University_of_Pennsylvania_College_Houses" title="University of Pennsylvania College Houses">University of Pennsylvania College Houses</a>
     </div> 
     <p>Every College House at the University of Pennsylvania has at least four members of faculty in the roles of House Dean, Faculty Master, and College House Fellows.<sup id="cite_ref-College_House_PDF_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-College_House_PDF-36"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> Within the College Houses, Penn has nearly 40 themed residential programs for students with shared interests such as world cinema or science and technology. Many of the nearby homes and apartments in the area surrounding the campus are often rented by undergraduate students moving off campus after their first year, as well as by graduate and professional students.</p> 
     <p>The College Houses include W.E.B. Du Bois, Fisher Hassenfeld, Gregory, Harnwell, Harrison, Hill, Kings Court English, Riepe, Rodin, Stouffer, and Ware.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> Fisher Hassenfeld, Ware, and Riepe together make up one building called &quot;The Quad.&quot;</p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Academics">Academics</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Pennsylvania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Academics">edit</a>]</span></h2>

     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:PENN_043.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/60/PENN_043.jpg/220px-PENN_043.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/60/PENN_043.jpg/330px-PENN_043.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/60/PENN_043.jpg/440px-PENN_043.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:PENN_043.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Lower Quad in winter, from Riepe College House
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The <a href="/wiki/University_of_Pennsylvania_School_of_Arts_and_Sciences" title="University of Pennsylvania School of Arts and Sciences">College of Arts and Sciences</a> is the undergraduate division of the School of Arts and Sciences, which also contains the Graduate Division and the College of Liberal and Professional Studies, Penn's division for non-traditional undergraduate and graduate students. <a href="/wiki/Wharton_School_of_the_University_of_Pennsylvania" title="Wharton School of the University of Pennsylvania">Wharton</a> is the business school of the University of Pennsylvania. Other undergraduate schools include the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Pennsylvania_School_of_Nursing" title="University of Pennsylvania School of Nursing">School of Nursing</a> and the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Pennsylvania_School_of_Engineering_and_Applied_Science" title="University of Pennsylvania School of Engineering and Applied Science">School of Engineering and Applied Science (SEAS)</a></p> 
     <p>Penn has a strong focus on interdisciplinary learning and research. It offers joint-degree programs, unique majors, and academic flexibility. Penn's &quot;One University&quot; policy allows undergraduates access to courses at all of Penn's undergraduate and graduate schools, except the medical, veterinary and dental schools. Undergraduates at Penn may also take courses at <a href="/wiki/Bryn_Mawr_College" title="Bryn Mawr College">Bryn Mawr</a>, <a href="/wiki/Haverford_College" title="Haverford College">Haverford</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Swarthmore_College" title="Swarthmore College">Swarthmore</a>, under a reciprocal agreement known as the <a href="/wiki/Quaker_Consortium" title="Quaker Consortium">Quaker Consortium</a>.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coordinated_dual-degree_and_interdisciplinary_programs">Coordinated dual-degree and interdisciplinary programs</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Pennsylvania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Coordinated dual-degree and interdisciplinary programs">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>Penn offers specialized <a href="/wiki/Double_degree" title="Double degree">coordinated dual-degree</a> (CDD) programs, which award candidates degrees from multiple schools at the University upon completion of graduation criteria of both schools. Undergraduate programs include:</p> 
     <ul> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/The_Jerome_Fisher_Program_in_Management_and_Technology" title="The Jerome Fisher Program in Management and Technology" class="mw-redirect">The Jerome Fisher Program in Management and Technology</a></li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/The_Huntsman_Program_in_International_Studies_and_Business" title="The Huntsman Program in International Studies and Business" class="mw-redirect">The Huntsman Program in International Studies and Business</a></li> 
      <li>Nursing and Health Care Management</li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Vagelos_Program_in_Life_Sciences_and_Management" title="Vagelos Program in Life Sciences and Management">The Roy and Diana Vagelos Program in Life Sciences and Management</a></li> 
      <li>Vagelos Integrated Program in Energy Research (VIPER)</li> 
      <li>Accelerated 7 year Bio-Dental Program</li> 
     </ul> 
     <p>Dual-degree programs which lead to the same multiple degrees without participation in the specific above programs are also available. Unlike CDD programs, &quot;dual degree&quot; students fulfill requirements of both programs independently without involvement of another program. Specialized dual-degree programs include Liberal Studies and Technology as well as a Computer and Cognitive Science Program. Both programs award a degree from the College of Arts and Sciences and a degree from the School of Engineering and Applied Sciences. In addition, the <a href="/wiki/Vagelos_Scholars_Program_in_Molecular_Life_Sciences" title="Vagelos Scholars Program in Molecular Life Sciences">Vagelos Scholars Program in Molecular Life Sciences</a> allows its students to either double major in the sciences or submatriculate and earn both a B.A. and a M.S. in four years. The most recent Vagelos Integrated Program in Energy Research (VIPER) will be first offered for the class of 2016. A joint program of Penn?s School of Arts and Sciences and the School of Engineering and Applied Science, VIPER leads to dual Bachelor of Arts and Bachelor of Science in Engineering degrees by combining majors from each school.</p> 
     <p>For graduate programs, Penn offers many formalized joint-degree graduate degrees such as a joint J.D./MBA, and maintains a list of interdisciplinary institutions, such as the Institute for Medicine and Engineering, the Joseph H. Lauder Institute for Management and International Studies, and the Institute for Research in Cognitive Science.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Academic_medical_center_and_biomedical_research_complex">Academic medical center and biomedical research complex</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Pennsylvania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Academic medical center and biomedical research complex">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tleft" style="width: 512px;"> 
      <div class="thumbinner"> 
       <div style="float: left; margin: 1px; width: 229px"> 
        <div class="thumbimage">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Hamilton_Walk_at_the_Perelman_School_of_Medicine.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Hamilton Walk and the John Morgan Building at the Perelman School of Medicine" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Hamilton_Walk_at_the_Perelman_School_of_Medicine.jpg/227px-Hamilton_Walk_at_the_Perelman_School_of_Medicine.jpg" width="227" height="150" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Hamilton_Walk_at_the_Perelman_School_of_Medicine.jpg/341px-Hamilton_Walk_at_the_Perelman_School_of_Medicine.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Hamilton_Walk_at_the_Perelman_School_of_Medicine.jpg/454px-Hamilton_Walk_at_the_Perelman_School_of_Medicine.jpg 2x" /></a>
        </div> 
        <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
         <a href="/wiki/Perelman_School_of_Medicine" title="Perelman School of Medicine" class="mw-redirect">Perelman School of Medicine</a>
        </div> 
       </div> 
       <div style="float: left; margin: 1px; width: 271px"> 
        <div class="thumbimage">
         <a href="/wiki/File:PennDentalSchool.jpg" class="image"><img alt="University of Pennsylvania Dental School" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e0/PennDentalSchool.jpg/269px-PennDentalSchool.jpg" width="269" height="150" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e0/PennDentalSchool.jpg/404px-PennDentalSchool.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e0/PennDentalSchool.jpg/538px-PennDentalSchool.jpg 2x" /></a>
        </div> 
        <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
         <a href="/wiki/University_of_Pennsylvania_School_of_Dental_Medicine" title="University of Pennsylvania School of Dental Medicine">University of Pennsylvania School of Dental Medicine</a>
        </div> 
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Penn's health-related programs?including the Schools of Nursing, Medicine, Dental Medicine, and Veterinary Medicine, and programs in bioengineering (School of Engineering) and health management (the Wharton School)?are among the university's strongest academic components. The combination of intellectual breadth, research funding (each of the health sciences schools ranks in the top 5 in annual NIH funding), clinical resources and overall scale ranks Penn with only a small handful of peer universities in the U.S.</p> 
     <p>The size of Penn's biomedical research organization, however, adds a very capital intensive component to the university's operations, and introduces revenue instability due to changing government regulations, reduced federal funding for research, and <a href="/wiki/Medicaid" title="Medicaid">Medicaid</a>/<a href="/wiki/Medicare_(United_States)" title="Medicare (United States)">Medicare</a> program changes. This is a primary reason highlighted in bond rating agencies' views on Penn's overall financial rating, which ranks one notch below its academic peers. Penn has worked to address these issues by pooling its schools (as well as several hospitals and clinical practices) into the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Pennsylvania_Health_System" title="University of Pennsylvania Health System">University of Pennsylvania Health System</a>, thereby pooling resources for greater efficiencies and research impact.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Admissions_selectivity">Admissions selectivity</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Pennsylvania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Admissions selectivity">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Princeton_Review" title="Princeton Review" class="mw-redirect">Princeton Review</a></i> ranks Penn as the 6th most selective school in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup> For the Class of 2015, entering in the fall of 2011, the University received a record of 31,659 applications and admitted 12.26 percent of the applicants (9.5% in the regular decision cycle), marking Penn's most selective admissions cycle in the history of the University.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Atlantic" title="The Atlantic">The Atlantic</a></i> also ranked Penn among the 10 most selective schools in the country. At the graduate level, Penn's admissions rates, like most universities', vary considerably based on school and program. Based on admission statistics from <i>U.S. News and World Report</i>, Penn's most selective programs include its law school, the health care schools (medicine, dental medicine, nursing, and veterinary), and its business school.</p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Research.2C_innovations.2C_and_discoveries">Research, innovations, and discoveries</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Pennsylvania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Research, innovations, and discoveries">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Entrance_to_Logan_Hall.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Entrance_to_Logan_Hall.jpg/220px-Entrance_to_Logan_Hall.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Entrance_to_Logan_Hall.jpg/330px-Entrance_to_Logan_Hall.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Entrance_to_Logan_Hall.jpg/440px-Entrance_to_Logan_Hall.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Entrance_to_Logan_Hall.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Claudia Cohen Hall, formerly Logan Hall, home of the College of Arts and Sciences and former home of the Wharton School
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Penn is considered a &quot;very high research activity&quot; university.<sup id="cite_ref-RU.2FVH_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RU.2FVH-49"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup> Its economic impact on the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania for 2010 amounted to $14 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2011 Penn topped the Ivy League in research expenditures with $814&nbsp;million worth of research,<sup id="cite_ref-Penn:_Penn_Facts_3-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Penn:_Penn_Facts-3"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nsf.gov_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nsf.gov-51"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> of which about 70% comes from federal support and in the most part from the Department of Health and Human Services.<sup id="cite_ref-Research_statistics_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Research_statistics-52"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup> Penn also enjoys strong support from the private sector, which in 2010 contributed almost $400&nbsp;million to Penn, making it the 6th strongest US university in terms of fundraising.<sup id="cite_ref-Council_for_Aid_to_Education_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Council_for_Aid_to_Education-53"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup> In line with its well-known interdisciplinary tradition, Penn's research centers often span two or more disciplines. In the 2010?11 academic year alone 5 interdisciplinary research centers were created or substantially expanded; these include the Center for Health-care Financing,<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup> the Center for Global Women?s Health at the Nursing School,<sup id="cite_ref-Nursing_Goes_Global_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nursing_Goes_Global-55"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup> the $13&nbsp;million Morris Arboretum?s Horticulture Center,<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> the $15&nbsp;million Jay H. Baker Retailing Center at Wharton,<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup> and the $13&nbsp;million Translational Research Center at Penn Medicine.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup> With these additions, Penn now counts 165 research centers hosting a research community of over 4,000 faculty and over 1,100 postdoctoral fellows, 5,400 academic support staff and graduate student trainees.<sup id="cite_ref-Penn:_Penn_Facts_3-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Penn:_Penn_Facts-3"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup> To further assist the advancement of interdisciplinary research President <a href="/wiki/Amy_Gutmann" title="Amy Gutmann">Amy Gutmann</a> established the &quot;Penn Integrates Knowledge&quot; title awarded to selected Penn professors &quot;whose research and teaching exemplify the integration of knowledge.&quot;<sup id="cite_ref-PIK_Professors_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PIK_Professors-59"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup> These professors hold endowed professorships and joint appointments between Penn's schools. The most recent of the 13 PIK professors is <a href="/wiki/Ezekiel_Emanuel" title="Ezekiel Emanuel">Ezekiel Emanuel</a>, who started at Penn in September 2011 as the Diane S. Levy and Robert M. Levy University Professor with a joint appointment at the Department of Medical Ethics &amp; Health Policy, which he chairs in the <a href="/wiki/Perelman_School_of_Medicine_at_the_University_of_Pennsylvania" title="Perelman School of Medicine at the University of Pennsylvania">Perelman School of Medicine</a>, and the Department of Health Care Management in the Wharton School.<sup id="cite_ref-PIK_Professors_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PIK_Professors-59"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>As a powerful research-oriented institution Penn is also among the most prolific and high-quality producers of doctoral students. With 487 PhDs awarded in 2009, Penn ranks third in the Ivy League, only behind <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University" title="Columbia University">Columbia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cornell" title="Cornell" class="mw-redirect">Cornell</a> (Harvard did not report data).<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup> It also has one of the highest numbers of post-doctoral appointees (933 in number for 2004?07), ranking third in the Ivy League (behind Harvard and Yale), and tenth nationally.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup> In most disciplines Penn professors' productivity is among the highest in the nation, and first in the fields of Epidemiology, Business, Communication Studies, Comparative Literature, Languages, Information Science, Criminal Justice and Criminology, Social Sciences and Sociology.<sup id="cite_ref-chronicle.com_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chronicle.com-12"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_National_Research_Council" title="United States National Research Council">National Research Council</a> nearly three-quarters of Penn?s 41 assessed programs were placed in ranges including the top 10 rankings in their fields, with more than half of these in ranges including the top 5 rankings in these fields.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Eniac.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Eniac.jpg/220px-Eniac.jpg" width="220" height="168" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Eniac.jpg/330px-Eniac.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Eniac.jpg/440px-Eniac.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Eniac.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> 
        <a href="/wiki/ENIAC" title="ENIAC">ENIAC</a>, the first general-purpose electronic computer, was born at Penn in 1946
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Penn's research tradition has historically been complemented by innovations that shaped higher education. In addition to establishing the first medical school, the first university teaching hospital, the first business school, and the first student union, Penn was also the cradle of other significant developments. In 1852 Penn Law was the first law school in the nation to publish a law journal still in existence (then called <i>The American Law Register,</i> now the <i><a href="/wiki/University_of_Pennsylvania_Law_Review" title="University of Pennsylvania Law Review">Penn Law Review</a></i>, one of the most cited law journals in the world).<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup> Under the deanship of <a href="/wiki/William_Draper_Lewis" title="William Draper Lewis">William Draper Lewis</a>, the law school was also one of the first schools to emphasize legal teaching by full-time professors instead of practitioners, a system that is still followed today.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup> The Wharton School was home to several pioneering developments in business education. It established the first research center in a business school in 1921 and the first center for entrepreneurship center in 1973,<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup> and it regularly introduced novel curricula for which <i><a href="/wiki/BusinessWeek" title="BusinessWeek" class="mw-redirect">BusinessWeek</a></i> wrote, &quot;Wharton is on the crest of a wave of reinvention and change in management education.&quot;<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Several major scientific discoveries have also taken place at Penn. The university is probably best well known as the place where the first general-purpose electronic computer (<a href="/wiki/ENIAC" title="ENIAC">ENIAC</a>) was born in 1946 at the <a href="/wiki/Moore_School_of_Electrical_Engineering" title="Moore School of Electrical Engineering">Moore School of Electrical Engineering</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Moore_Engineering_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moore_Engineering-68"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup> It was here also where the world's first spelling and grammar checkers were created, as well as the popular <a href="/wiki/COBOL" title="COBOL">COBOL</a> programming language.<sup id="cite_ref-Moore_Engineering_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moore_Engineering-68"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup> Penn can also boast some of the most important discoveries in the field of medicine. The <a href="/wiki/Dialysis" title="Dialysis">dialysis</a> machine used as an artificial replacement for lost kidney function was conceived and devised out of a pressure cooker by William Inouye while he was still a student at Penn Med;<sup id="cite_ref-Medical_Innovations_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Medical_Innovations-69"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Rubella" title="Rubella">Rubella</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_B" title="Hepatitis B">Hepatitis B</a> vaccines were developed at Penn;<sup id="cite_ref-Medical_Innovations_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Medical_Innovations-69"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup> the discovery of cancer's link with genes, <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_therapy" title="Cognitive therapy">cognitive therapy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Retin-A" title="Retin-A" class="mw-redirect">Retin-A</a> (the cream used to treat acne), <a href="/wiki/Resistin" title="Resistin">Resistin</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_gene" title="Philadelphia gene" class="mw-redirect">Philadelphia gene</a> (linked to <a href="/wiki/Chronic_myelogenous_leukemia" title="Chronic myelogenous leukemia">chronic myelogenous leukemia</a>), and the technology behind <a href="/wiki/Positron_Emission_Tomography" title="Positron Emission Tomography" class="mw-redirect">PET Scans</a> were all discovered by Penn Med researchers.<sup id="cite_ref-Medical_Innovations_69-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Medical_Innovations-69"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup> More recent gene research has led to the discovery of the genes for <a href="/wiki/Fragile_X_syndrome" title="Fragile X syndrome">fragile X syndrome</a>, the most common form of inherited mental retardation, <a href="/wiki/Kennedy%27s_disease" title="Kennedy's disease" class="mw-redirect">Kennedy's disease</a>, a disorder marked by progressive muscle and bulbar atrophy, and <a href="/wiki/Charcot%E2%80%93Marie%E2%80%93Tooth_disease" title="Charcot?Marie?Tooth disease">Charcot?Marie?Tooth disease</a>, a progressive neurodegenerative disease that affects the hands, feet, and limbs.<sup id="cite_ref-Medical_Innovations_69-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Medical_Innovations-69"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Conductive_polymer" title="Conductive polymer">Conductive polymer</a> was also developed at Penn by <a href="/wiki/Alan_J._Heeger" title="Alan J. Heeger">Alan J. Heeger</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alan_MacDiarmid" title="Alan MacDiarmid">Alan MacDiarmid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hideki_Shirakawa" title="Hideki Shirakawa">Hideki Shirakawa</a>, an invention that earned them the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Chemistry" title="Nobel Prize in Chemistry">Nobel Prize in Chemistry</a>. <a href="/wiki/Ralph_L._Brinster" title="Ralph L. Brinster">Ralph L. Brinster</a>, on faculty since 1965, developed the scientific basis for <a href="/wiki/In_vitro_fertilization" title="In vitro fertilization" class="mw-redirect">in vitro fertilization</a> and the transgenic mouse at Penn. The theory of <a href="/wiki/BCS_theory" title="BCS theory">superconductivity</a> was also partly developed at Penn, by then faculty member <a href="/wiki/John_Robert_Schrieffer" title="John Robert Schrieffer">John Robert Schrieffer</a> (along with <a href="/wiki/John_Bardeen" title="John Bardeen">John Bardeen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Leon_Cooper" title="Leon Cooper">Leon Cooper</a>). The university has also contributed major advancements in the fields of economics and management. Among the many discoveries are <a href="/wiki/Conjoint_analysis" title="Conjoint analysis">conjoint analysis</a>, widely used as a predictive tool especially in market research, <a href="/wiki/Simon_Kuznets" title="Simon Kuznets">Simon Kuznets</a>'s method of measuring <a href="/wiki/Gross_National_Product" title="Gross National Product" class="mw-redirect">Gross National Product</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-125_Influential_People_and_Ideas_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125_Influential_People_and_Ideas-71"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Penn_effect" title="Penn effect">Penn effect</a> (the observation that consumer price levels in richer countries are systematically higher than in poorer ones), and the &quot;Wharton Model&quot;<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup> developed by Nobel-laurete <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_Klein" title="Lawrence Klein">Lawrence Klein</a> to measure and forecast economic activity. The idea behind <a href="/wiki/Health_Maintenance_Organization" title="Health Maintenance Organization" class="mw-redirect">Health Maintenance Organizations</a> also belonged to Penn professor Robert Eilers, who put it into practice during then President Nixon's health reform in the 1970s.<sup id="cite_ref-125_Influential_People_and_Ideas_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125_Influential_People_and_Ideas-71"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Rankings">Rankings</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Pennsylvania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Rankings">edit</a>]</span></h2>
